Í∞ïÎèàÍµ¨. (2012). Ïú†Íµê Ï°∞ÏÉÅÏùòÎ°ÄÏùò ÎØ∏Îûò. Ï¢ÖÍµêÏó∞Íµ¨, 69, 1-26.

  1. Were there any significant changes in the education system during this decade that concerns religion? (e.g., Introduction of secular ideas in formal curriculum)

: Only upper class, Yangban, learned Confucian classics (Êº¢Â≠∏) in the traditional education. The missionaries brought new form of education to Joseon. The missionary school focused on training young men in three areas: mental, moral, and spiritual. The first students of the mission schools were mostly from lower class, Cheonmin Ë≥§Ê∞ëand Sangmin Â∏∏Ê∞ë. The primacy of Bai-chai (ÂüπÊùê) college and other mission schools was the teaching of Christ. Bunker explained it this way:

The brief outline does not take into consideration the constant personal influence of the Christian teacher upon the scholar, nor the wholesome uplift given to a crude boy by breathing the atmosphere of a Christian institution. Nor in this connection should mention of the nature of our textbooks be omitted. Our books are edited on strictly religious principles. They contain prayers, and many helpful hints as to God, His goodness, power and love as to Christ and his power to save all who call upon Him. There are many thoughts along these and similar lines which tend to turn the minds of the students into proper channels. One of our boys closed a letter to the writer with a benediction quoted from St. Paul‚Äôs writings. Not a bad beginning for a Bai-Chai boy. Certainly, a god ending. (1896, p. 361. See the box)

Jaisohn and other progressive Christian leaders served as teachers in Bai-chai high school and college and many of those students got involved in Hyeopsunghoe (ÂçîÊàêÊúÉ) and the Independence Club. These associations fought official corruption, inefficiency, and timidity toward foreign aggressiveness.

Women played a vital role in the education of Korean girls. Methodist Mary Scranton founded her school in June 1886 in Seoul with a single pupil, a child found lying against the city wall with her mother who was suffering from typhus (Clark, 2003). Scranton‚Äôs school became known as Ewha which was the first educational institution for women. Because of Ewha and other schools, a Korea woman‚Äôs life experienced a revolution. No longer was she reduced to the duties of reverence to ancestors and hospitality to guests. For once, the monopoly on knowledge did not belong solely to men for knowledge became just as important to them as to men for the full enjoyment and development of life.

  1. Please describe the selected country/society‚Äôs overall diplomatic relationship with its neighboring Asian-Pacific countries (500-1000 words).

You may organize the description with the following sensitizing questions. (1) With what countries did it have positive relationship in the sense that they form some forms of cooperation/alliance in politics, economic, cultural, or military spheres? (2) With what countries did it have negative relationship in the sense that they were in some forms of tension, conflict, war, invasion/occupation/colonization in various spheres? (3) With what countries did it have neutral relationship?

: Korea is a peninsula located between Japan on the east, the Korea Straits on the south and the Yellow Sea on the west. To the north, it is separated from China by the Amnok and Tuman Rivers. Eleven miles of the Tuman River from a border with Russia. The geographical setting of Korea made it the likely prey of its neighbors. Korea was attached by Japan in 1592 and the Manchurians in 1636. From then on, the country never completely recovered and followed isolationism. During the nineteenth century, the political system became corrupt as practically to preclude securing justice. During this period, the door of Korea was forced open, and Daewon gun implemented an intensive close-door policy as his son, Go-jong, came to throne. The political power game between Daewon gun and his daughter-in-law, Queen Min, reshaped relationships with foreign countries during the late nineteenth century. Daewon gun maintained diplomatic relations with only Qing Dynasty and ostracized Western powers and Japan (1852-1876). This political stance resulted in General Sherman Incident with the U.S. (1866), the French campaign against Korea (‰∏ôÂØÖÊ¥ãÊìæ) of 1866, rejection to Japanese‚Äô sovereign‚Äôs message (1868), Korea Expedition with the U.S. (1871) and the following persecutions for Christians, mostly Catholics. He built the anti-appeasement steles (Êñ•ÂíåÁ¢ë) to remember his principles to denounce the western and Japanese power. However, his reform failed to get rid of corrupted officials and construct a strong country. Queen Min incarcerated him in a small palace. Go-jong and Queen Min signed treaties open the door to Japan in 1876 and the U.S. in 1882. Her administration committee positive relationships with Japan and western countries to hold the Russia‚Äôs expansion at the beginning. However, she lost the balance of her political directions among foreign powers and internal social and political issues. Compared to Daewon gun, she overly dependent on Qing dynasty and Japan to suppress Imo Gullan (Â£¨ÂçàËªç‰∫Ç) and Donghak peasant revolution (Êù±Â≠∏Ëæ≤Ê∞ëÈù©ÂëΩ). This intervention resulted in First Sino-Japanese War (1894).

The onset of the Sino-Japanese war on the first of August 1894 devasted the Korean land and plunged the Korean people into a welter of terror until it reached the Shimonoseki Treaty on April 17 in 1895. Chinese military was routed by Japan and lost its imperial power in Korea. In the midst of the war, Japan pressed the Korean government to change structurally by recalling the scattered progressives. The reputed political traitors of the Gap-sin coup in 1884 were able to return to Korea around this time. Philip Jaisohn and Chi-ho Yun (who will be introduced in the next decade section) came back to Korea. Japan subsequently installed a task-oriented supra-government reform organ, the Deliberative Council (Kun‚Äôguk Kimuch‚Äôo, ËªçÂúãÊ©üÂãôËôï), largely manned by those progressives that seemingly implemented, what the progressives had originally sought. The reinstated Korean progressives and the reform, a measure welcomed by the progressives, were in fact a long-term instrument to break up the Korean leadership. Japan supported Buddhism, Shintoism, and other forms of Japanese religions in Seoul to compete against Christianity to expand Japanese power in the East Asia (Weems, 1962).

  1. Please list the primary 3 English and/or native Asian language bibliographical sources that documented the diplomatic relationship with its neighboring countries during this decade.

  2. Please describe the selected country/society‚Äôs overall relationship with some major Christian-majority countries, such as USA, Canada, and European countries (500-1000 words).

:As stated above, Christian-majority countries were ostracized until Go-jong and Queen Min took their administration power from Daewon gun in 1876. The first step was to sign the United States-Korea Treaty in 1882. This treaty established mutual friendship and mutual assistance in case of attack; and the treaty also addressed such specific matters as extraterritorial rights for U.S. citizens in Korea and most favored nation trade status. This template was modeled in treaties with European nations such as Germany in 1883, Russia and Italy in 1884, and France in 1886. It is doubtful to define these relationships were positive, neutral, or negative at that time. At least, Joseon dynasty was not prepared to open the nation‚Äôs door.

  3. Nationalism can be expressed in interaction rituals that create a ‚Äúnationalist aesthetics‚Äù that leads people to become emotionally invested in the nation. A shared emotion produced by a nationalist ritual can generate a powerful emotional energy that produces strong feelings of solidarity, confidence, and righteousness among a group of people who are connected symbolically. These strong feelings may be transformed into anger and violent actions against those who disrespect the group (Collins 1975, 1999, 2004; Kane and Park 2009).

Based on the above-mentioned description of nationalist rituals, can you identify and briefly describe any nationalist rituals that took place during this decade? (e.g., the 1919 March First Movement in Korea; the 1919 May Fourth Movement in China, etc.)

: Manmingongdonghoe (People‚Äôs Joint Association, Ëê¨Ê∞ëÂÖ±ÂêåÊúÉ) and Gwanmingondonghoe (Joint Conference of Government Officials and the People, ÂÆòÊ∞ëÂÖ±ÂêåÊúÉ). The Independence Club held regular debates that were opened to the public. It debated various topics using parliamentary procedure so that all voices could have a hearing and communication could be established on an equal footing. When this debating club discussed corruption, independence, injustice, and inequality. It began to become a locus of political agitation. In 1898, Jaisohn organized mass demonstrations to demand various changes from the government. This marked the beginning of the Manmingongdonghoe. This mobilization of broad support for political demands inevitably made enemies among both government officials and foreign diplomats, especially the Russians, who then enjoyed an unrivaled influence on the Korean government. Pro-Christian leaders wer often elected to represent this association. Besides drawing its leadership from the Christian community, this association also had strong support among the Christians, who were more open to reformist values and ideology than any other group. The association between pro-Christian leaders and the Manmingongdonghoe was further demonstrated by the independence movement.

Please describe religious exchanges or conflicts with religious organizations of other countries (e.g., Japanese Buddhists visiting China during the Cultural Revolution, the World Buddhist Forum hosted by China in the 2000s, Korean missionaries incidents, the Unification Church expansion and political activities in Japan, etc. or any collaboration in suppressing ‚Äúcults‚Äù or New Religions)

  2. Were there any significant changes in the education system during this decade that concerns religion?

  3. Did the government prosecute individuals or organizations because of religion during this decade? 

The Baby Riots of 1888 took place in the summer of 1888 in Joseon Korea. It was reported by Dr. L. Horton Underwood, one of the first female medical doctors, as well as female missionaries in Korea, and the wife of Rev. Horace G. Underwood, the first American Presbyterian missionary to the country. The uproar against Western medicine, hospitals and foreign schools-and foreigners in general-came just a few years after the first American Protestant missionaries started their work in Joseon.

‚ÄúSome person or persons, with malicious intent, started a rumor which spread like wildfire that foreigners were paying wicked Koreans to steal native children in order to cut their hearts and eyes out to be used for medicine‚Ä¶.. Babies, it was said, had been eaten at the German, English, and Americal legation, and the hospital, of course was considered by all the headquarters of this bloodthirsty work, for there, where medicine was manufactured and diseases threated, the babies must certainly be butchered.‚Äù (Underwood, 1904, see the Box).

For some weeks, the excitement among the people became so great that the lives and property of foreigners, especially missionaries, were thought to be in peril (Scranton et al., 1888). Finally, the rumor provoked a riot in which several Koreans died. Rumors of the barbarous crimes of missionaries and foreigners fueled a furious mob that assaulted a Korean office on street and gathered to attack a foreign school for women founded by an American Methodist missionary, M.F. Scranton, who recalled that, ‚ÄòOne day a crowd gathered outside our gate and accused our servants of having stolen children and sold them to me for money, and threatened to kill them‚Äô (Scranton et al., 1888). In addition to this incident, on other occasions crowds gathered about the gate and threatened to kill the servants: ‚ÄôGuards were appointed, who vigilantly patrolled our compounds and we held ourselves, in accordance with the direction of our U.S. Minister, in constant readiness to leave at a moment‚Äôs notice; (Scranton, 1896). In view of these persistent attempts to attack the compound, missionaries had to be ready to leave at a moment‚Äôs notice. At the peak of the riot, Scranton also had to close the women‚Äôs school temporarily. Eventually, this incident brought about the diplomatic and military intervention of the USA, France and Russia, because foreigners and missionaries in general were accused of evil behaviors.

  1. Did the government persecute individuals or organizations because of religion? 

1894 Pyongyang Persecution

An instance of persecution in Pyongyang in 1894, shortly before the Donghak Rebellion, provides a good case in point of anti-foreign and anti-Christian atmosphere. At that time, Pyongyang was not a treaty city and foreign residence was not permitted there, much less the Christian religion. Yet, both the Presbyterian and Methodist missionaries had visited this city and made several converts there. Their justification for their activities was that the Japanese were already there and that Americans should deserve the same rights of residence as citizens of a most-favored-nation. Moreover, they purchased property in the name of local converts and made it into a clandestine church. However, the illegal Christian intrusion was soon detected by local residents and reported to the local authorities. The governor of Pyongyang quickly arrested and jailed the native Christians who had helped the foreigners, while the missionaries were saved from the same fate due to their extraterritorial status. Missionaries responded to local Christian requests ‚Äúto secure protection for them,‚Äù by sending an urgent telegraph to the American and British legations in Seoul to press the Korean Foreign Ministry to release the Christians in Pyongyang. The Korean government responded quickly, but the governor, who possessed considerable power as a close relative of the current Queen, resisted the release order from the central government and continued torturing the Christians in prison. To make matters worse, the missionary residence in the city was sieged by a local anti-Christian mob. The house was stoned and the wall was torn down, while native water carriers were blocked from supplying water to the foreigners. In the vent, the governor surrendered to the central government and the Korean Christians were released, but the Pyongyang Persecution in 1894 provides further proof that there was no official or legal sanction for Christian mission work in Korea.

  1. Please summarize the overall level of social hostilities toward Christianity during this decade.

Despite the real sense that Joseon government was turning a blind eye toward missionary work, its optimism was nevertheless unfounded and did not reflect the reality of the foreign mission in Korea. The extension of Protestant mission work without official government sanctioning caused as many political complications with Korean government and society as the Catholics. The Protestant followed the Catholic‚Äôs lead to justify its illegal mission work under the unequal treaty and there was no difference between the two missions in terms of their neglect of Korean sovereignty and local jurisdiction.

In fact, anti-missionary opposition was conspicuous from the very beginning of the Protestant missions. When the Presbyterian mission began a hospital and an orphanage in Seoul, a vicious rumor soon spread among the local populace that the missionaries were sending them Koreans to America as Alaves and fattening them up for the table (Underwood, 1886). In 1887, street shops in Seoul closed in unison-a traditional means of expressing popular dissatisfaction-and Seoul merchants demanded the removal of all foreigners from the city center to suburban Yongsan, outside the city gate.

: Protestant Christianity was incompatible not merely with the stubborn defenders of the old Confucian system but also with the alternative attempt of the Donghak to revise certain elements of the traditional system. It was difficult to find to associate Christians themselves with the Donghak followers.

  5. Was there any tension between nationalism/patriotism and Christianity in general?

Nationalism can be expressed in interaction rituals that create a ‚Äúnationalist aesthetics‚Äù that leads people to become emotionally invested in the nation. A shared emotion produced by a nationalist ritual can generate a powerful emotional energy that produces strong feelings of solidarity, confidence, and righteousness among a group of people who are connected symbolically. These strong feelings may be transformed into anger and violent actions against those who disrespect the group (Collins 1975, 1999, 2004; Kane and Park 2009).

  1. Based on the above-mentioned description of nationalist rituals, can you identify and briefly describe any nationalist rituals that took place during this decade?

Following up the ‚ÄúJoint Conference of Government Officials and the People‚Äù, the Independence club held the ‚ÄúJoint Conference for all People‚Äù (Ëê¨Ê∞ëÂÖ±ÂêåÊúÉ) to condemn military interventions by Russia. About 10,000 Seoul citizen participated in this rally and this conference was spread out to other province.

Please describe religious exchanges or conflicts with religious organizations of other countries (e.g., Japanese Buddhists visiting China during the Cultural Revolution, the World Buddhist Forum hosted by China in the 2000s, Korean missionaries incidents, the Unification Church expansion and political activities in Japan, etc. or any collaboration in suppressing ‚Äúcults‚Äù or New Religions)

  2. Did any level of government impose any level of restrictions on proselytizing by foreign clergy or missionaries. (This includes denial of visas if it is specifically aimed at missionaries but not if it is the same type applicable to any foreigner.)

Land and property ownership in Korea between the 1880s-1900s was somewhat chaotic. Due in part to locals‚Äô unpreparedness, the situation was brought on by opportunistic foreigners who pushed the limits of law and treaty regulations. The issue of foreigners buying up real estate was even a problem in the center of Seoul. By the 1900s, English-language reports show that the private sale or lease of real estate to Japanese, Chinese, and Western migrants outside of designated settlement treaty areas or treaty regulations had become rampant.

Ï£ºÏùº Î¨ºÏùòÎÇ†ÏùÑ ÏùºÍ≥±Í∞ÄÏßÄÏõêÏπôÏóê ÏùòÍ±∞ÌïòÏó¨ Ï†úÏ†ï. Í∞úÏã†ÍµêÏùò ÏòÅÌñ•ÏùÑ Î∞õÏùå ÏïàÏãù

Relationship between Donghak and Christianity (Catholicism, Protestantism) cannot be simply viewed as a relationship of rejection. Donghak, which emerged with the motto ‚ÄúCheokwaeyang‚Äù Êñ•ÂÄ≠Ê¥ã (Rejecting Western and Japanese Influence) advocated by Choi Je-u who began the propagation in 1861, marked a significant milestone in Korean modern national history around the 1890s. Donghak incorporated Confucianism, Buddhism, Taoism, and folk beliefs as its core, and emerged as a national religion for a short period, rapidly spreading among the people even while facing suppression of Catholicism‚Äôs missionary activities.

Due to Choi Je-u‚Äôs use of the term ‚ÄúCheonju‚Äù Â§©‰∏ª (Heavenly Lord), Donghak was initially misunderstood as a branch of Western learning and faced persecution.

Relationship with Catholicism: Catholicism was referred to as ‚ÄúSeohak‚Äù (Western Learning). Despite being criticized as a religion of foreign powers and facing persecution due to ancestral rites issues and the concept of ‚ÄúMubumugun‚Äù ÁÑ°Áà∂ÁÑ°Âêõ (No Father, No Lord), the number of believers increased due to the involvement of the common people. However, several severe persecutions exacerbated social turmoil. Hence, Donghak intentionally emphasized its distinction from Seohak and Sahak ÈÇ™Â≠∏ (evil learning).

‚ÄúIt was called Donghak, Êù±Â≠∏ meaning Eastern Learning of Dongguk Êù±Âúã (East Country). And Seohak, Western Learning, is yin Èô∞, while Donghak is yang ÈôΩ, aiming to suppress yin with yang. Westerners lack order in speech, coherence in writing, and are solely interested in personal gain through evil tricks, lacking the Ê∞£Âåñ transformation in their bodies and the teachings of the heavens in their learning; they have only form but no essence. By not revering the heavens, Western learning is close to emptiness, and the learning has departed from the teachings of the heavens.‚Äù (Donggyeong Daejeon Nonhak Mun, p. 264)

Choi Je-u expressed caution towards the Catholic attitude towards ancestral rites. However, he himself faced criticism for adhering to Western learning. The initial stance of Catholics towards Donghak seems to have been marked by resentment by early French missionaries, likely due to the harsh persecution Donghak forces inflicted upon Catholic believers during the Donghak Revolution of 1894 in Jeonju and Gongju (Yoon Chi-ho‚Äôs Diary). However, an indicator of a possibly favorable relationship is the case of An Jung-geun, a Catholic who resonated with the rejection of foreign influence and joined Donghak followers in attacking a prison together in Northwestern areas.

Relationship with Protestantism: Initially, it was a relationship of rejection, especially with significant church damages in the southern Honam region. However, in the northwest, a common goal of anti-Japanese struggle was established. While Catholicism held an overall conflictual relationship, Protestantism formed different relationships in the southern and northwestern regions respectively. Particularly, McKenzie, a Canadian Presbyterian missionary in the northwest, engaged in missionary work while living among Koreans in traditional small, thatched homes and caring for the poor. Despite the Donghak forces‚Äô combat in this region, records show that Donghak military was impressed by him and received baptism.

An example of rejection and protest occurred in the ‚ÄúBokhap Sangso‚Äù ‰ºèÈñ§‰∏äÁñè petition at Gwanghwamun in March 1893. It was a protest against the Joseon government‚Äôs recognition of Catholicism and Protestantism while refusing to acknowledge Donghak. Christian rejection notices were posted on American consulate buildings and missionary homes. The missionaries were criticized for acts like conducting unauthorized schools, collecting donations, and warned to return to their own country, with the accusation of pursuing self-interest. Joseon‚Äôs protection shielded the missionaries from harm, but efforts to understand each other were present in areas like Soraesa Church in the northern region where McKenzie and Reverend Seo Gyeong-jo were active. The church also offered protection to Donghak forces (January 1895). This cooperative effort extended with Son Byeong-hee forming the Progressive Society and Christian Seo Sang-ryun becoming its leader, eventually leading to the March 1 Movement.

Lastly, the exchange of faith practices between Protestantism and Donghak is explained through the concepts of temperament and morning prayer (Hwang, 2014). Donghak followers adhere to five principles: spells Ï£ºÎ¨∏(Âë™Êñá), clear water Ï≤≠Ïàò(Ê∑∏Ê∞¥), Holy Lord‚Äôs day ÏãúÏùº(‰æçÊó•), rice offering ÏÑ±ÎØ∏(Ë™†Á±≥), and prayer Í∏∞ÎèÑ(Á•àÁ¶±). Clear water refers to the blood spilled when Choi Je-u died, and it symbolizes washing the body. Holy Lord‚Äôs day signifies Sunday, which in Protestantism is considered a holy day. Rice offering involves collecting a spoonful of rice from each person‚Äôs meal, saved up and offered to the church at the end of each month. Morning prayer refers to prayers done upon waking up in the morning.
